Main articles: [Arab immigration to the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_immigration_to_the_United_States "Arab immigration to the United States"), [Arab Americans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Americans "Arab Americans"), [Arab Americans in Metro Detroit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Americans_in_Metro_Detroit "Arab Americans in Metro Detroit"), [History of the Middle Eastern people in Metro Detroit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Middle_Eastern_people_in_Metro_Detroit "History of the Middle Eastern people in Metro Detroit"), [Arab Canadians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Canadians "Arab Canadians"), [Arab Brazilians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Brazilians "Arab Brazilians"), [Arab Mexicans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Mexicans "Arab Mexicans"), [Arab Chileans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Chileans "Arab Chileans"), [Arab Colombians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Colombians "Arab Colombians"), [Arab Uruguayans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Uruguayans "Arab Uruguayans"), [Arab Venezuelans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Venezuelans "Arab Venezuelans"), [Arab Cubans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Cubans "Arab Cubans"), [Arab Haitians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Haitians "Arab Haitians"), [Arab immigration to Honduras](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_immigration_to_Honduras "Arab immigration to Honduras"), [Arab Argentines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Argentines "Arab Argentines"), [Lebanese Paraguayans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lebanese_Paraguayans "Lebanese Paraguayans"), and [Arab Uruguayans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Uruguayans "Arab Uruguayans")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Gigi_Hadid_Vogue_2022_02.jpg/128px-Gigi_Hadid_Vogue_2022_02.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gigi_Hadid_Vogue_2022_02.jpg)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Steve_Jobs_Headshot_2010-CROP2.jpg/106px-Steve_Jobs_Headshot_2010-CROP2.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Steve_Jobs_Headshot_2010-CROP2.jpg)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Shakira_for_VOGUE_in_2021_2.png/116px-Shakira_for_VOGUE_in_2021_2.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Shakira_for_VOGUE_in_2021_2.png)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Salma_Hayek_Deauville_2012.jpg/118px-Salma_Hayek_Deauville_2012.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Salma_Hayek_Deauville_2012.jpg)

[Gigi Hadid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gigi_Hadid "Gigi Hadid") is a model and television personality, [Steve Jobs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steve_Jobs "Steve Jobs") was the co-founder, chairman, and CEO of Apple, [Shakira](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shakira "Shakira") is a singer and songwriter and [Salma Hayek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salma_Hayek "Salma Hayek") is an actress and film producer.

[Arab immigration to the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_immigration_to_the_United_States "Arab immigration to the United States") began in larger numbers during the 1880s, and today, an estimated 3.7 million Americans have some Arabic background.<sup id="cite_ref-aaiusa.org_15-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-aaiusa.org-15">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-327"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-327">[324]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-328"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-328">[325]</a></sup> [Arab Americans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Americans "Arab Americans") are found in every state, but more than two thirds of them live in just ten states, and one-third live in [Los Angeles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Los_Angeles "Los Angeles"), [Detroit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Detroit "Detroit"), and [New York City](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_York_City "New York City") specifically.<sup id="cite_ref-aaiusa.org_15-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-aaiusa.org-15">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-329"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-329">[326]</a></sup> Most Arab Americans were born in the US, and nearly 82% of US-based Arabs are citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated42_330-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-autogenerated42-330">[327]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-aai.3cdn.net2_331-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-aai.3cdn.net2-331">[328]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-332"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-332">[329]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-333"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-333">[330]</a></sup>

Arabs immigrants began to arrive in [Canada](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canada "Canada") in small numbers in 1882. Their [immigration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immigration "Immigration") was relatively limited until 1945, after which time it increased progressively, particularly in the 1960s and thereafter.<sup id="cite_ref-334"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-334">[331]</a></sup> According to the website "Who are [Arab Canadians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Canadians "Arab Canadians")", [Montreal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Montreal "Montreal"), the Canadian city with the largest Arab population, has approximately 267,000 Arab inhabitants.<sup id="cite_ref-335"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-335">[332]</a></sup>

[Latin America](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latin_America "Latin America") has the largest Arab population outside of the [Arab World](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_World "Arab World").<sup id="cite_ref-336"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-336">[333]</a></sup> Latin America is home to anywhere from 17–25 to 30 million people of Arab descent, which is more than any other diaspora region in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-337"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-337">[334]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-338"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-338">[335]</a></sup> The [Brazilian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Federal_government_of_Brazil "Federal government of Brazil") and Lebanese governments claim there are 7 million [Brazilians of Lebanese descent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lebanese_Brazilians "Lebanese Brazilians").<sup id="cite_ref-itamaraty12_339-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-itamaraty12-339">[336]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-libano12_340-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-libano12-340">[337]</a></sup> Also, the Brazilian government claims there are 4 million [Brazilians of Syrian descent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syrian_Brazilians "Syrian Brazilians").<sup id="cite_ref-itamaraty12_339-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-itamaraty12-339">[336]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IBGE2008_7-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-IBGE2008-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-saudiaramcoworld12_341-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-saudiaramcoworld12-341">[338]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-dsbra2_342-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-dsbra2-342">[339]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-343"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-343">[340]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-344"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-344">[341]</a></sup> Other large Arab communities includes [Argentina](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argentina "Argentina") (about 3,500,000<sup id="cite_ref-Fearab.org.ar_16-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Fearab.org.ar-16">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-oni.escuelas.edu.ar2_345-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-oni.escuelas.edu.ar2-345">[342]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-346"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-346">[343]</a></sup>)

The interethnic marriage in the Arab community, regardless of religious affiliation, is very high; most community members have only one parent who has Arab ethnicity.<sup id="cite_ref-347"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-347">[344]</a></sup> [Colombia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colombia "Colombia") (over 3,200,000<sup id="cite_ref-348"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-348">[345]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-349"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-349">[346]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-350"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-350">[347]</a></sup>), [Venezuela](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Venezuela "Venezuela") (over 1,600,000),<sup id="cite_ref-thedailybeast.com_27-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-thedailybeast.com-27">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-351"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-351">[348]</a></sup> [Mexico](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mexico "Mexico") (over 1,100,000),<sup id="cite_ref-352"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-352">[349]</a></sup> [Chile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chile "Chile") (over 800,000),<sup id="cite_ref-353"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-353">[350]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-354"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-354">[351]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-select.nytimes.com2_355-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-select.nytimes.com2-355">[352]</a></sup> and [Central America](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_America "Central America"), particularly [El Salvador](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/El_Salvador "El Salvador"), and [Honduras](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Honduras "Honduras") (between 150,000 and 200,000).<sup id="cite_ref-The_Arabs_of_Honduras2_356-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-The_Arabs_of_Honduras2-356">[353]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Adnkronos.com_33-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Adnkronos.com-33">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Laventana.casa.cult.cu_34-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Laventana.casa.cult.cu-34">[33]</a></sup> [Arab Haitians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Haitians "Arab Haitians") (257,000<sup id="cite_ref-357"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-357">[354]</a></sup>) a large number of whom live in the [capital](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Port-au-Prince "Port-au-Prince") are more often than not, concentrated in financial areas where the majority of them establish businesses.<sup id="cite_ref-al-shorfa.com2_358-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-al-shorfa.com2-358">[355]</a></sup>

#### Caucasus

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Caucasus_1060_map_en.svg/200px-Caucasus_1060_map_en.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Caucasus_1060_map_en.svg)

Georgia and the Caucasus in 1060, during the final decline of the emirate

In 1728, a Russian officer described a group of Arab nomads who populated the [Caspian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caspian_Sea "Caspian Sea") shores of [Mughan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mughan_plain "Mughan plain") (in present-day [Azerbaijan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Azerbaijan "Azerbaijan")) and spoke a mixed Turkic-Arabic language.<sup id="cite_ref-Genko2_359-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Genko2-359">[356]</a></sup> It is believed that these groups migrated to the [South Caucasus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Caucasus "South Caucasus") in the 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-zelkina2_360-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-zelkina2-360">[357]</a></sup> The 1888 edition of [Encyclopædia Britannica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica "Encyclopædia Britannica") also mentioned a certain number of Arabs populating the [Baku Governorate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baku_Governorate "Baku Governorate") of the [Russian Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Empire "Russian Empire").<sup id="cite_ref-Baynesp5142_361-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Baynesp5142-361">[358]</a></sup> They retained an [Arabic dialect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shirvani_Arabic "Shirvani Arabic") at least into the mid-19th century,<sup id="cite_ref-Bakik2_362-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Bakik2-362">[359]</a></sup> there are nearly 30 settlements still holding the name _Arab_ (for example, [Arabgadim](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C6%8Fr%C9%99bq%C9%99dim "Ərəbqədim"), [Arabojaghy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C6%8Fr%C9%99boca%C4%9F%C4%B1 "Ərəbocağı"), [Arab-Yengija](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C6%8Fr%C9%99byengic%C9%99 "Ərəbyengicə"), etc.). From the time of the Arab conquest of the [South Caucasus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Caucasus "South Caucasus"), continuous small-scale Arab migration from various parts of the Arab world occurred in [Dagestan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dagestan "Dagestan"). The majority of these lived in the village of Darvag, to the north-west of [Derbent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derbent "Derbent"). The latest of these accounts dates to the 1930s.<sup id="cite_ref-zelkina2_360-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-zelkina2-360">[357]</a></sup> Most Arab communities in southern Dagestan underwent linguistic [Turkicisation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkification "Turkification"), thus nowadays Darvag is a majority-[Azeri](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Azerbaijanis "Azerbaijanis") village.<sup id="cite_ref-Sefer2_363-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Sefer2-363">[360]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wrum2_364-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Wrum2-364">[361]</a></sup>

#### Central, South, East and Southeast Asia

According to the _History of Ibn Khaldun_, the Arabs that were once in [Central Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Asia "Central Asia") have been either killed or have fled the Tatar invasion of the region, leaving only the locals.<sup id="cite_ref-Khaldun2_365-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Khaldun2-365">[362]</a></sup> However, today many people in Central Asia identify as Arabs. Most [Arabs of Central Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Asian_Arabic "Central Asian Arabic") are fully integrated into local populations, and sometimes call themselves the same as locals (for example, [Tajiks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/T%C4%81jik_people "Tājik people"), [Uzbeks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uzbeks "Uzbeks")) but they use special titles to show their Arab origin such as [Sayyid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sayyid "Sayyid"), [Khoja](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khoja_(Turkestan) "Khoja (Turkestan)") or [Siddiqui](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Siddiqui "Siddiqui").<sup id="cite_ref-Owensp1842_366-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Owensp1842-366">[363]</a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Ketchimalai_Mosque-_Beruwala%2C_Sri_Lanka.jpg/200px-Ketchimalai_Mosque-_Beruwala%2C_Sri_Lanka.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ketchimalai_Mosque-_Beruwala,_Sri_Lanka.jpg)

The mosque is built at the spot where the first Arab traders landed and subsequently settled in the area.<sup id="cite_ref-367"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-367">[364]</a></sup>

There are only two communities in [India](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/India "India") which claim Arab descent, the [Chaush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chaush "Chaush") of the [Deccan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deccan_Plateau "Deccan Plateau") region and the [Chavuse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_(Gujarat) "Arab (Gujarat)") of [Gujarat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gujarat "Gujarat").<sup id="cite_ref-368"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-368">[365]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-369"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-369">[366]</a></sup> These groups are largely descended from [Hadhrami](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hadhrami_people "Hadhrami people") migrants who settled in these two regions in the 18th century. However, neither community still speaks Arabic, although the Chaush have seen re-immigration to [Eastern Arabia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Arabia "Eastern Arabia") and thus a re-adoption of Arabic.<sup id="cite_ref-370"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-370">[367]</a></sup> In [South Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Asia "South Asia"), where Arab ancestry is considered prestigious, some communities have origin myths that claim Arab ancestry. Several communities following the [Shafi'i madhab](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shafi%CA%BDi_school "Shafiʽi school") (in contrast to other [South Asian Muslims](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islam_in_South_Asia "Islam in South Asia") who follow the [Hanafi madhab](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hanafi "Hanafi")) claim descent from Arab traders like the [Konkani Muslims](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Konkani_Muslims "Konkani Muslims") of the [Konkan region](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Konkan "Konkan"), the [Mappilla](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mappilla "Mappilla") of [Kerala](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kerala "Kerala"), and the [Labbai](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Labbai "Labbai") and [Marakkar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marakkar "Marakkar") of [Tamil Nadu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tamil_Nadu "Tamil Nadu") and a few Christian groups in India that claim and have Arab roots are situated in the state of [Kerala](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kerala "Kerala").<sup id="cite_ref-371"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-371">[368]</a></sup> South Asian [Iraqi biradri](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iraqi_Biradari "Iraqi Biradari") may have records of their ancestors who migrated from [Iraq](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iraq "Iraq") in historical documents. The [Sri Lankan Moors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sri_Lankan_Moors "Sri Lankan Moors") are the third largest ethnic group in [Sri Lanka](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sri_Lanka "Sri Lanka"), constituting 9.23% of the country's total population.<sup id="cite_ref-372"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-372">[369]</a></sup> Some sources trace the ancestry of the Sri Lankan [Moors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moors "Moors") to Arab traders who settled in Sri Lanka at some time between the 8th and 15th centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-TheSundayTimes2_373-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-TheSundayTimes2-373">[370]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Anthropos2_374-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Anthropos2-374">[371]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Islamic_Studies2_375-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Islamic_Studies2-375">[372]</a></sup> There are about 5,000,000 [Native Indonesians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Native_Indonesians "Native Indonesians") with Arab ancestry,<sup id="cite_ref-376"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-376">[373]</a></sup> of [Hadrami](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hadhrami_people "Hadhrami people") descent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECribbKahin200418–19_377-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-FOOTNOTECribbKahin200418%E2%80%9319-377">[374]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECribbKahin200418–19_377-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-FOOTNOTECribbKahin200418%E2%80%9319-377">[374]</a></sup>

#### Sub-Saharan Africa

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Baggara_belt.png/300px-Baggara_belt.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Baggara_belt.png)

Map of the Baggara belt

[Afro-Arabs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afro-Arabs "Afro-Arabs") are individuals and groups from [Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Africa "Africa") who are of partial Arab descent. Most Afro-Arabs inhabit the [Swahili Coast](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Swahili_coast "Swahili coast") in the [African Great Lakes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/African_Great_Lakes "African Great Lakes") region, although some can also be found in parts of the Arab world.<sup id="cite_ref-Romero2_378-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Romero2-378">[375]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gunnar2_379-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Gunnar2-379">[376]</a></sup> Large numbers of Arabs migrated to [West Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Africa "West Africa"), particularly [Côte d'Ivoire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/C%C3%B4te_d%27Ivoire "Côte d'Ivoire") (home to over 100,000 Lebanese),<sup id="cite_ref-380"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-380">[377]</a></sup> [Senegal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Senegal "Senegal") (roughly 30,000 Lebanese),<sup id="cite_ref-381"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-381">[378]</a></sup> [Sierra Leone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sierra_Leone "Sierra Leone") (roughly 10,000 Lebanese today; about 30,000 prior to the outbreak of [civil war](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sierra_Leone_Civil_War "Sierra Leone Civil War") in 1991), [Liberia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liberia "Liberia"), and [Nigeria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nigeria "Nigeria").<sup id="cite_ref-382"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-382">[379]</a></sup> Since the end of the civil war in 2002, [Lebanese](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lebanese_people "Lebanese people") traders have become re-established in Sierra Leone.<sup id="cite_ref-383"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-383">[380]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-384"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-384">[381]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-385"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-385">[382]</a></sup> The Arabs of Chad occupy northern Cameroon and Nigeria (where they are sometimes known as Shuwa), and extend as a belt across Chad and into Sudan, where they are called the [Baggara](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baggara "Baggara") grouping of [Arab](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab "Arab") ethnic groups inhabiting the portion of Africa's [Sahel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sahel "Sahel"). There are 171,000 in [Cameroon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cameroon "Cameroon"), 150,000 in [Niger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Niger "Niger")<sup id="cite_ref-386"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-386">[383]</a></sup>), and 107,000 in the [Central African Republic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_African_Republic "Central African Republic").<sup id="cite_ref-387"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-387">[384]</a></sup>

## Religion

Arabs are mostly Muslims with a [Sunni](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sunni_Islam "Sunni Islam") majority and a [Shia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia_Islam "Shia Islam") minority, one exception being the [Ibadis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ibadi "Ibadi"), who predominate in [Oman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oman "Oman").<sup id="cite_ref-ahmadi2_388-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-ahmadi2-388">[385]</a></sup> [Arab Christians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Christians "Arab Christians") generally follow [Eastern Churches](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Churches "Eastern Churches") such as the [Greek Orthodox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_Orthodox "Greek Orthodox") and [Greek Catholic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_Catholic "Greek Catholic") churches, though a minority of [Protestant Church](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protestant_Church "Protestant Church") followers also exists.<sup id="cite_ref-Christi2_389-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Christi2-389">[386]</a></sup> There are also Arab communities consisting of [Druze](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Druze "Druze") and [Baháʼís](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith "Baháʼí Faith").<sup id="cite_ref-390"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-390">[387]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-391"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-391">[388]</a></sup> Historically, there were also sizeable populations of [Arab Jews](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Jews "Arab Jews") around the Arab World.

[Before the coming of Islam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Religion_in_pre-Islamic_Arabia "Religion in pre-Islamic Arabia"), most Arabs followed a pagan religion with a number of deities, including [Hubal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubal "Hubal"),<sup id="cite_ref-Hubal2_392-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Hubal2-392">[389]</a></sup> [Wadd](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wadd "Wadd"), [Allāt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-l%C4%81t "Al-lāt"),<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated12_393-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-autogenerated12-393">[390]</a></sup> [Manat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Man%C4%81t "Manāt"), and [Uzza](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Uzza "Al-Uzza"). A few individuals, the _[hanifs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hanif "Hanif")_, had apparently rejected [polytheism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polytheism "Polytheism") in favor of [monotheism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monotheism "Monotheism") unaffiliated with any particular religion. Some tribes had converted to Christianity or Judaism. The most prominent Arab Christian kingdoms were the [Ghassanid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghassanid "Ghassanid") and [Lakhmid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lakhmid "Lakhmid") kingdoms.<sup id="cite_ref-Marib2_394-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Marib2-394">[391]</a></sup> When the [Himyarite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Himyarite "Himyarite") king converted to [Judaism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Judaism "Judaism") in the late 4th century,<sup id="cite_ref-Hima2_395-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Hima2-395">[392]</a></sup> the elites of the other prominent Arab kingdom, the [Kindites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kindites "Kindites"), being Himyirite vassals, apparently also converted (at least partly). With the expansion of Islam, polytheistic Arabs were rapidly [Islamized](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamization "Islamization"), and polytheistic traditions gradually disappeared.<sup id="cite_ref-MSNU2_396-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-MSNU2-396">[393]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EncyRe2_397-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-EncyRe2-397">[394]</a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/The_Ka%27ba%2C_Great_Mosque_of_Mecca%2C_Saudi_Arabia_%284%29.jpg/180px-The_Ka%27ba%2C_Great_Mosque_of_Mecca%2C_Saudi_Arabia_%284%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:The_Ka%27ba,_Great_Mosque_of_Mecca,_Saudi_Arabia_(4).jpg)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Church_of_the_Nativity_%287703592746%29.jpg/204px-Church_of_the_Nativity_%287703592746%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Church_of_the_Nativity_(7703592746).jpg)

Today, Sunni Islam dominates in most areas, vastly so in Levant, North Africa, West Africa and the Horn of Africa. Shia Islam is dominant in [Bahrain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bahrain "Bahrain") and southern [Iraq](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iraq "Iraq") while northern Iraq is mostly Sunni. Substantial Shia populations exist in [Lebanon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lebanon "Lebanon"), [Yemen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yemen "Yemen"), [Kuwait](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kuwait "Kuwait"), [Saudi Arabia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saudi_Arabia "Saudi Arabia"),<sup id="cite_ref-Shia2_398-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Shia2-398">[395]</a></sup> northern [Syria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syria "Syria") and Al-Batinah Region in [Oman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oman "Oman"). There are small numbers of [Ibadi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ibadi "Ibadi") and [non-denominational Muslims](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Non-denominational_Muslims "Non-denominational Muslims") too.<sup id="cite_ref-ahmadi2_388-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-ahmadi2-388">[385]</a></sup> The [Druze](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Druze "Druze") community is concentrated in Levant.<sup id="cite_ref-399"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-399">[396]</a></sup>

Christianity had a prominent presence In [pre-Islamic Arabia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pre-Islamic_Arabia "Pre-Islamic Arabia") among several Arab communities, including the [Bahrani people](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bahrani_people "Bahrani people") of [Eastern Arabia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Arabia "Eastern Arabia"), the [Christian community of Najran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christian_community_of_Najran "Christian community of Najran"), in parts of [Yemen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yemen#Ancient_history "Yemen"), and among certain northern Arabian tribes such as the [Ghassanids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghassanids "Ghassanids"), [Lakhmids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lakhmids "Lakhmids"), [Taghlib](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taghlib "Taghlib"), [Banu Amela](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Banu_Amela "Banu Amela"), [Banu Judham](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Banu_Judham "Banu Judham"), [Tanukhids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tanukhids "Tanukhids") and [Tayy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tayy "Tayy"). In the early Christian centuries, Arabia was sometimes known as _Arabia heretica_, due to its being "well known as a breeding-ground for heterodox interpretations of Christianity."<sup id="cite_ref-400"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-400">[397]</a></sup> Christians make up 5.5% of the population of Western Asia and North Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-Pacini2_401-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Pacini2-401">[398]</a></sup> In Lebanon, Christians number about 40.5% of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_World_Factbook:_Lebanon2_402-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-CIA_World_Factbook:_Lebanon2-402">[399]</a></sup> In Syria, Christians make up 10% of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_World_Factbook:_Syria2_403-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-CIA_World_Factbook:_Syria2-403">[400]</a></sup> [Christians in Palestine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palestinian_Christians "Palestinian Christians") make up 8% and 0.7% of the populations, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-FactbookGazaStrip2_404-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-FactbookGazaStrip2-404">[401]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FactbookWestBank2_405-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-FactbookWestBank2-405">[402]</a></sup> In Egypt, Christians number about 10% of the population. In Iraq, Christians constitute 0.1% of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-406"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-406">[403]</a></sup>

In Israel, Arab Christians constitute 2.1% (roughly 9% of the Arab population).<sup id="cite_ref-WFIS2_407-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-WFIS2-407">[404]</a></sup> Arab Christians make up 8% of the population of [Jordan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jordan "Jordan").<sup id="cite_ref-WFJord2_408-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-WFJord2-408">[405]</a></sup> Most [North](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_America "North America") and [South American](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_America "South America") Arabs are Christian,<sup id="cite_ref-AAIUSA2_409-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-AAIUSA2-409">[406]</a></sup> so are about half of the Arabs in [Australia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia "Australia") who come particularly from Lebanon, Syria and Palestine. One well known member of this religious and ethnic community is [Saint Abo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abo_of_Tiflis "Abo of Tiflis"), martyr and the patron saint of [Tbilisi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tbilisi "Tbilisi"), [Georgia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georgia_(country) "Georgia (country)").<sup id="cite_ref-410"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-410">[407]</a></sup> Arab Christians also live in holy Christian cities such as [Nazareth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nazareth "Nazareth"), [Bethlehem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bethlehem "Bethlehem") and the [Christian Quarter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christian_Quarter "Christian Quarter") of the [Old City of Jerusalem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_City_of_Jerusalem "Old City of Jerusalem") and many other villages with holy Christian sites.

## Culture

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Illustration_by_David_Roberts%2C_digitally_enhanced_by_rawpixel-com_120.jpg/220px-Illustration_by_David_Roberts%2C_digitally_enhanced_by_rawpixel-com_120.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Illustration_by_David_Roberts,_digitally_enhanced_by_rawpixel-com_120.jpg)

Dancing girls at Cairo illustration by David Roberts (1796–1864).

Arab culture is shaped by a long and rich history that spans thousands of years, from the [Atlantic Ocean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean "Atlantic Ocean") in the west to the [Arabian Sea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabian_Sea "Arabian Sea") in the east, and from the [Mediterranean Sea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea "Mediterranean Sea") in the north to the Horn of Africa and the Indian Ocean in the southeast. The various religions the Arabs have adopted throughout [their history](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Arabs "History of the Arabs") and the various empires and kingdoms that have ruled and took lead of the Arabic civilization have contributed to the [ethnogenesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethnogenesis "Ethnogenesis") and formation of modern Arab culture. [Language](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Language "Language"), [literature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Literature "Literature"), [gastronomy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gastronomy "Gastronomy"), [art](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Art "Art"), [architecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Architecture "Architecture"), [music](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Music "Music"), [spirituality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spirituality "Spirituality"), [philosophy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophy "Philosophy") and [mysticism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mysticism "Mysticism") are all part of the cultural heritage of the Arabs.<sup id="cite_ref-411"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-411">[408]</a></sup>

### Language

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Learning_Arabic_calligraphy.jpg/200px-Learning_Arabic_calligraphy.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Learning_Arabic_calligraphy.jpg)

[Arabic calligraphy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic_calligraphy "Arabic calligraphy") is the artistic practice of writing Arabic script in a decorative and stylized manner.

[Arabic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic "Arabic") is a [Semitic language](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Semitic_language "Semitic language") of the [Afro-Asiatic Family](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages "Afroasiatic languages").<sup id="cite_ref-412"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-412">[409]</a></sup> The first evidence for the emergence of the language appears in military accounts from 853 BCE. Today it has developed widely used as a _[lingua franca](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lingua_franca "Lingua franca")_ for more than 500 million people. It is also a [liturgical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liturgy#Islam "Liturgy") language for 1.7 billion [Muslims](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muslims "Muslims").<sup id="cite_ref-pewmuslim422_413-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-pewmuslim422-413">[410]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-414"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-414">[411]</a></sup> Arabic is one of six [official languages of the United Nations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Official_languages_of_the_United_Nations "Official languages of the United Nations"),<sup id="cite_ref-415"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-415">[412]</a></sup> and is revered in [Islam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islam "Islam") as the language of the [Quran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quran "Quran").<sup id="cite_ref-pewmuslim422_413-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-pewmuslim422-413">[410]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-416"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-416">[413]</a></sup>

Arabic has two main registers. [Classical Arabic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_Arabic "Classical Arabic") is the form of the [Arabic language](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic_language "Arabic language") used in literary texts from [Umayyad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate "Umayyad Caliphate") and Abbasid times (7th to 9th centuries). It is based on the medieval dialects of [Arab tribes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tribes_of_Arabia "Tribes of Arabia"). [Modern Standard Arabic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_Standard_Arabic "Modern Standard Arabic") (MSA) is the direct descendant used today throughout the Arab world in writing and in formal speaking, for example, prepared speeches, some radio broadcasts, and non-entertainment content,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBin-Muqbil200614_417-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBin-Muqbil200614-417">[414]</a></sup> while the [lexis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lexis_(linguistics) "Lexis (linguistics)") and [stylistics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stylistics_(linguistics) "Stylistics (linguistics)") of [Modern Standard Arabic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_Standard_Arabic "Modern Standard Arabic") are different from [Classical Arabic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_Arabic "Classical Arabic"). There are also various regional dialects of colloquial spoken Arabic that both vary greatly from both each other and from the formal written and spoken forms of Arabic.<sup id="cite_ref-418"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-418">[415]</a></sup>

### Mythology

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Illustration_of_Aladdin_Flying_Away_with_Two_People_from_the_Arabian_Nights.jpg/170px-Illustration_of_Aladdin_Flying_Away_with_Two_People_from_the_Arabian_Nights.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Illustration_of_Aladdin_Flying_Away_with_Two_People_from_the_Arabian_Nights.jpg)

[Aladdin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aladdin "Aladdin") from the _[One Thousand and One Nights](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/One_Thousand_and_One_Nights "One Thousand and One Nights")_

[Arabic mythology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic_mythology "Arabic mythology") comprises the ancient beliefs of the Arabs. Prior to Islam the Kaaba of Mecca was covered in symbols representing the myriad demons, djinn, demigods, or simply tribal gods and other assorted deities which represented the polytheistic culture of pre-Islamic. It has been inferred from this plurality an exceptionally broad context in which mythology could flourish.<sup id="cite_ref-419"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-419">[416]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-420"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-420">[417]</a></sup>

The most popular beasts and demons of Arabian mythology are [Bahamut](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bahamut "Bahamut"), [Dandan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dandan "Dandan"), [Falak](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Falak_(Arabian_legend) "Falak (Arabian legend)"), [Ghoul](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghoul "Ghoul"), [Hinn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hinn_(mythology) "Hinn (mythology)"), [Jinn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jinn "Jinn"), [Karkadann](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karkadann "Karkadann"), [Marid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marid "Marid"), [Nasnas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nasnas "Nasnas"), [Qareen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Qareen "Qareen"), [Roc](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roc_(mythology) "Roc (mythology)"), [Shadhavar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shadhavar "Shadhavar"), [Werehyena](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Werehyena "Werehyena") and other assorted creatures which represented the profoundly [polytheistic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polytheistic "Polytheistic") environment of pre-Islamic.<sup id="cite_ref-421"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-421">[418]</a></sup>

The most prominent symbol of Arabian mythology is the [Jinn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jinn "Jinn") or genie.<sup id="cite_ref-422"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-422">[419]</a></sup> Jinns are supernatural beings that can be good or evil.<sup id="cite_ref-423"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-423">[420]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-424"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-424">[421]</a></sup> They are not purely spiritual, but are also physical in nature, being able to interact in a tactile manner with people and objects and likewise be acted upon. The _jinn_, humans, and [angels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_view_of_angels "Islamic view of angels") make up the known [sapient](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sapience "Sapience") creations of [God](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/God_in_Islam "God in Islam").<sup id="cite_ref-425"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-425">[422]</a></sup>

[Ghouls](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghoul "Ghoul") also feature in the mythology as a [monster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monster "Monster") or [evil spirit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evil_spirit "Evil spirit") associated with graveyards and consuming human flesh.<sup id="cite_ref-426"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-426">[423]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-427"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-427">[424]</a></sup> In Arabic folklore, ghouls belonged to a diabolic class of jinn and were said to be the offspring of Iblīs, the prince of darkness in Islam. They were capable of constantly changing form, but always retained [donkey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Donkey "Donkey")'s hooves.<sup id="cite_ref-428"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-428">[425]</a></sup>

### Literature

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Khalili_Collection_Islamic_Art_MSS_0902-61b-62a.jpg/220px-Khalili_Collection_Islamic_Art_MSS_0902-61b-62a.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Khalili_Collection_Islamic_Art_MSS_0902-61b-62a.jpg)

Manuscript from the _Diwan_ of [Al-Mutanabbi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Mutanabbi "Al-Mutanabbi")

The [Quran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quran "Quran"), the main [holy book](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Religious_text "Religious text") of [Islam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islam "Islam"), had a significant influence on the Arabic language, and marked the beginning of Arabic literature. Muslims believe it was transcribed in the Arabic dialect of the [Quraysh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quraysh "Quraysh"), the tribe of [Muhammad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muhammad "Muhammad").<sup id="cite_ref-Glen_Van_Brummelen-2013_429-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Glen_Van_Brummelen-2013-429">[426]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-430"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-430">[427]</a></sup> As Islam spread, the Quran had the effect of unifying and standardizing Arabic.<sup id="cite_ref-Glen_Van_Brummelen-2013_429-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Glen_Van_Brummelen-2013-429">[426]</a></sup>

Not only is the Quran the first work of any significant length written in the language, but it also has a far more complicated structure than the earlier literary works with its 114 _[suwar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surah "Surah")_ (chapters) which contain 6,236 _[ayat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ayat "Ayat")_ (verses). It contains [injunctions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Injunction "Injunction"), [narratives](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Narrative "Narrative"), [homilies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homilies "Homilies"), [parables](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parable "Parable"), direct addresses from God, instructions and even comments on how the Quran will be received and understood. It is also admired for its layers of metaphor as well as its clarity, a feature which is mentioned in [An-Nahl](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/An-Nahl "An-Nahl"), the 16th surah.

[Al-Jahiz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Jahiz "Al-Jahiz") (born 776, in [Basra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basra "Basra") – December 868/January 869) was an Arab prose writer and author of works of literature, [Mu'tazili](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mu%27tazili "Mu'tazili") theology, and politico-religious polemics. A leading scholar in the Abbasid Caliphate, his canon includes two hundred books on various subjects, including [Arabic grammar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic_grammar "Arabic grammar"), [zoology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zoology "Zoology"), poetry, lexicography, and [rhetoric](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhetoric "Rhetoric"). Of his writings, only thirty books survive. Al-Jāḥiẓ was also one of the first Arabian writers to suggest a complete overhaul of the language's grammatical system, though this would not be undertaken until his fellow linguist [Ibn Maḍāʾ](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ibn_Ma%E1%B8%8D%C4%81%CA%BE "Ibn Maḍāʾ") took up the matter two hundred years later.<sup id="cite_ref-431"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-431">[428]</a></sup>

There is a small remnant of [pre-Islamic poetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pre-Islamic_poetry "Pre-Islamic poetry"), but Arabic literature predominantly emerges in the [Middle Ages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_Ages "Middle Ages"), during the [Golden Age of Islam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Golden_Age_of_Islam "Golden Age of Islam").<sup id="cite_ref-432"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-432">[429]</a></sup> [Imru' al-Qais](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Imru%27_al-Qais "Imru' al-Qais") was a king and poet in the 6th century, he was the last king of [Kindite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kinda "Kingdom of Kinda"). He is among the finest Arabic poetry to date, as well sometimes considered the father of [Arabic poetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic_poetry "Arabic poetry").<sup id="cite_ref-433"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-433">[430]</a></sup> _[Kitab al-Aghani](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kitab_al-Aghani "Kitab al-Aghani")_ by [Abul-Faraj](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abu_al-Faraj_al-Isfahani "Abu al-Faraj al-Isfahani") was called by the 14th-century historian [Ibn Khaldun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ibn_Khaldun "Ibn Khaldun") the register of the Arabs.<sup id="cite_ref-434"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-434">[431]</a></sup> [Literary Arabic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_Standard_Arabic "Modern Standard Arabic") is derived from [Classical Arabic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_Arabic "Classical Arabic"), based on the language of the Quran as it was analyzed by [Arabic grammarians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic_grammarians "Arabic grammarians") beginning in the 8th century.<sup id="cite_ref-435"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-435">[432]</a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Khalil_Gibran_-_Autorretrato_con_musa%2C_c._1911.jpg/170px-Khalil_Gibran_-_Autorretrato_con_musa%2C_c._1911.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Khalil_Gibran_-_Autorretrato_con_musa,_c._1911.jpg)

[Khalil Gibran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khalil_Gibran "Khalil Gibran") was a writer, poet and visual artist; he is best known as the author of _[The Prophet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Prophet_(book) "The Prophet (book)")_, has since become one of the [best-selling books](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_best-selling_books "List of best-selling books") of all time, having been [translated into more than 100 languages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Translations_of_The_Prophet "Translations of The Prophet")

A large portion of Arabic literature before the 20th century is in the form of [poetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic_poetry "Arabic poetry"), and even prose from this period is either filled with snippets of poetry or is in the form of _[saj](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saj_(literature) "Saj (literature)")_ or rhymed prose.<sup id="cite_ref-436"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-436">[433]</a></sup> The _[ghazal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghazal "Ghazal")_ or love poem had a long history being at times tender and chaste and at other times rather explicit.<sup id="cite_ref-437"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-437">[434]</a></sup> In the [Sufi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sufi "Sufi") tradition the love poem would take on a wider, [mystical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mysticism "Mysticism") and [religious](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Religion "Religion") importance.

[Arabic epic literature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic_epic_literature "Arabic epic literature") was much less common than poetry, and presumably originates in oral tradition, written down from the 14th century or so. [Maqama](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maqama "Maqama") or [rhymed prose](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhymed_prose "Rhymed prose") is intermediate between poetry and [prose](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prose "Prose"), and also between fiction and non-fiction.<sup id="cite_ref-438"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-438">[435]</a></sup> [Maqama](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maqama "Maqama") was an incredibly popular form of Arabic literature, being one of the few forms which continued to be written during the decline of Arabic in the 17th and 18th centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-439"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-439">[436]</a></sup>

Arabic literature and [culture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_culture "Arab culture") declined significantly after the 13th century, to the benefit of [Turkish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_Turkish_language "Ottoman Turkish language") and [Persian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Persian_literature "Persian literature"). A modern revival took place beginning in the 19th century, alongside [resistance against Ottoman rule](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_awakenings_under_the_Ottoman_Empire "National awakenings under the Ottoman Empire"). The literary revival is known as _[al-Nahda](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Nahda "Al-Nahda")_ in Arabic, and was centered in [Egypt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egypt "Egypt") and [Lebanon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lebanon "Lebanon"). Two distinct trends can be found in the _nahda_ period of revival.<sup id="cite_ref-440"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-440">[437]</a></sup>

The first was a neo-classical movement which sought to rediscover the literary traditions of the past, and was influenced by traditional literary genres—such as the _[maqama](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maqama "Maqama")_—and works like _[One Thousand and One Nights](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/One_Thousand_and_One_Nights "One Thousand and One Nights")_. In contrast, a modernist movement began by translating Western modernist works—primarily novels—into Arabic.<sup id="cite_ref-441"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-441">[438]</a></sup> A tradition of [modern Arabic poetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_Arabic_poetry "Modern Arabic poetry") was established by writers such as [Francis Marrash](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Francis_Marrash "Francis Marrash"), [Ahmad Shawqi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ahmad_Shawqi "Ahmad Shawqi") and [Hafiz Ibrahim](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hafiz_Ibrahim "Hafiz Ibrahim"). Iraqi poet [Badr Shakir al-Sayyab](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Badr_Shakir_al-Sayyab "Badr Shakir al-Sayyab") is considered to be the originator of free verse in [Arabic poetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic_poetry "Arabic poetry").<sup id="cite_ref-442"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-442">[439]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-443"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-443">[440]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-444"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-444">[441]</a></sup>

### Cuisine

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Petra_metzes.jpg/220px-Petra_metzes.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Petra_metzes.jpg)

Plate of [Mezes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meze "Meze") in [Petra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Petra "Petra"), [Jordan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jordan "Jordan")

[Arab cuisine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_cuisine "Arab cuisine") is largely divided into [Khaleeji cuisine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khaleeji_cuisine "Khaleeji cuisine"), [Levantine cuisine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Levantine_cuisine "Levantine cuisine") and [Maghrebi cuisine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maghrebi_cuisine "Maghrebi cuisine").<sup id="cite_ref-445"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-445">[442]</a></sup> Arab cuisine has influenced other cuisines various cultures, including [Ottoman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_Empire "Ottoman Empire"), [Persian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iranian_peoples "Iranian peoples"), and [Andalusian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andalusian_Spanish "Andalusian Spanish").

It is characterized by a variety of herbs and spices, including [cumin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cumin "Cumin"), [coriander](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coriander "Coriander"), [cinnamon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cinnamon "Cinnamon"), [sumac](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sumac "Sumac"), [za'atar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Za%27atar "Za'atar"), [cardamom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardamom "Cardamom"), [mint](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mentha "Mentha"), [saffron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saffron "Saffron"), [sesame](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sesame "Sesame"), [thyme](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thyme "Thyme") [turmeric](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turmeric "Turmeric") and [parsley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parsley "Parsley").<sup id="cite_ref-Food_and_recipes2_446-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Food_and_recipes2-446">[443]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-447"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-447">[444]</a></sup> Arab cuisine is also known for its sweets and desserts, such as [Knafeh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knafeh "Knafeh"), [Baklava](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baklava "Baklava"), [Halva](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Halva "Halva"), and [Qatayef](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Qatayef "Qatayef"). [Arabic coffee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic_coffee "Arabic coffee"), or qahwa, is a traditional drink that is served with dates.

### Art

(1st row) Various examples of early Umayyad paintings in Qusayr 'Amra. (2nd row) Examples of Abbasid Figural paintings from Samarra. (3rd row) Examples of Fatimid art.

[Arabic art](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic_art "Arabic art") has taken various forms, including, among other things, [jewelry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jewelry "Jewelry"), [textiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Textile "Textile") and [architecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Architecture "Architecture").<sup id="cite_ref-448"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-448">[445]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-449"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-449">[446]</a></sup> Arabic script has also traditionally been heavily embellished with often colorful [Arabic calligraphy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic_calligraphy "Arabic calligraphy"), with one notable and widely used example being [Kufic script](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kufic_script "Kufic script").<sup id="cite_ref-450"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-450">[447]</a></sup> Arabic miniatures ([Arabic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic "Arabic"): الْمُنَمْنَمَات الْعَرَبِيَّة, _Al-Munamnamāt al-ʿArabīyah_) are small [paintings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Painting "Painting") on [paper](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paper "Paper"), usually book or manuscript illustrations but also sometimes separate artworks that occupy entire pages. The earliest example dates from around 690 CE, with a flourishing of the art from between 1000 and 1200 CE in the Abbasid caliphate. The art form went through several stages of evolution while witnessing the fall and rise of several [Arab caliphates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caliphate "Caliphate").

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Khalili_Collection_Hajj_and_Arts_of_Pilgrimage_kfq_0060.jpg/138px-Khalili_Collection_Hajj_and_Arts_of_Pilgrimage_kfq_0060.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Khalili_Collection_Hajj_and_Arts_of_Pilgrimage_kfq_0060.jpg)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Codex_Parisino-Petropolitanus_02.jpg/138px-Codex_Parisino-Petropolitanus_02.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Codex_Parisino-Petropolitanus_02.jpg)

Arab miniaturists got totally assimilated and subsequently disappeared due to the [Ottoman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_Empire "Ottoman Empire") occupation of the Arab world. Nearly all forms of Islamic miniatures ([Persian miniatures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Persian_miniature "Persian miniature"), [Ottoman miniatures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_miniature "Ottoman miniature") and [Mughal miniatures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mughal_miniature "Mughal miniature")) owe their existences to Arabic miniatures, as Arab patrons were the first to demand the production of illuminated manuscripts in the Caliphate, it was not until the 14th century that the artistic skill reached the non-Arab regions of the Caliphate.<sup id="cite_ref-451"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-451">[448]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DavidCollection2_452-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-DavidCollection2-452">[449]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MMA19332_453-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-MMA19332-453">[450]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-454"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-454">[451]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-455"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-455">[452]</a></sup>

Despite the considerable changes in Arabic miniature style and technique, even during their last decades, the early [Umayyad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate "Umayyad Caliphate") Arab influence could still be noticed. Arabic miniature artists include [Ismail al-Jazari](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ismail_al-Jazari "Ismail al-Jazari"), who illustrated his own _Book of Knowledge of Ingenious Mechanical Devices._<sup id="cite_ref-Jazari2_456-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Jazari2-456">[453]</a></sup>

The Abbasid artist, [Yahya Al-Wasiti](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yahya_ibn_Mahmud_al-Wasiti "Yahya ibn Mahmud al-Wasiti"), who probably lived in [Baghdad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baghdad "Baghdad") in the late Abbasid era (12th to 13th-centuries), was one of the pre-eminent exponents of the Baghdad school. In the period 1236–1237, he transcribed and illustrated the book _Maqamat_ (also known as the _Assemblies_ or the _Sessions_), a series of anecdotes of social satire written by [Al-Hariri of Basra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Hariri_of_Basra "Al-Hariri of Basra").<sup id="cite_ref-457"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-457">[454]</a></sup> The narrative concerns the travels of a middle-aged man as he uses his charm and eloquence to swindle his way across the Arabic world.<sup id="cite_ref-458"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-458">[455]</a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Egitto%2C_cairo%2C_placca_decorativa_in_avorio%2C_XI_sec_-_Louvre_-_OA_6265-1.jpg/238px-Egitto%2C_cairo%2C_placca_decorativa_in_avorio%2C_XI_sec_-_Louvre_-_OA_6265-1.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Egitto,_cairo,_placca_decorativa_in_avorio,_XI_sec_-_Louvre_-_OA_6265-1.jpg)

Arabesque pattern behind hunters on [ivory plaque](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ivory_carving "Ivory carving"), 11th–12th century, Egypt

With most surviving Arabic [manuscripts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manuscript "Manuscript") in western museums,<sup id="cite_ref-459"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-459">[456]</a></sup> Arabic miniatures occupy very little space in modern Arab culture.<sup id="cite_ref-460"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-460">[457]</a></sup> [Arabesque](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabesque "Arabesque") is a form of artistic decoration consisting of "surface decorations based on rhythmic linear patterns of scrolling and interlacing foliage, tendrils" or plain lines,<sup id="cite_ref-461"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-461">[458]</a></sup> often combined with other elements. Another definition is "Foliate ornament, typically using leaves, derived from stylised [half-palmettes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Half-palmette "Half-palmette"), which were combined with spiralling stems".<sup id="cite_ref-462"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-462">[459]</a></sup> It usually consists of a single design which can be 'tiled' or seamlessly repeated as many times as desired.<sup id="cite_ref-RobinsonIllustrated2_463-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-RobinsonIllustrated2-463">[460]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-464"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-464">[461]</a></sup>

### Architecture

The Arab world is home to around 8%<sup id="cite_ref-465"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-465">[462]</a></sup> of [UNESCO](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UNESCO "UNESCO") [World Heritage Sites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Heritage_Site "World Heritage Site") ([List of World Heritage Sites in the Arab states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_World_Heritage_Sites_in_the_Arab_states "List of World Heritage Sites in the Arab states")). The oldest examples of architecture include those of [pre-Islamic Arabia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pre-Islamic_Arabia "Pre-Islamic Arabia"),<sup id="cite_ref-:05222_466-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-:05222-466">[463]</a></sup> as well as [Nabataean architecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nabataean_architecture "Nabataean architecture") that developed in the ancient [kingdom of the Nabataeans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nabataean_Kingdom "Nabataean Kingdom"), a nomadic Arab tribe that controlled a significant portion of the [Middle East](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_East "Middle East") from the 4th century BCE to the 2nd century CE.<sup id="cite_ref-467"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-467">[464]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-468"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-468">[465]</a></sup> The Nabataeans were known for their skill in carving out elaborate buildings, tombs, and other structures from the sandstone cliffs of the region. One of the most famous examples of Nabataean architecture is the city of [Petra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Petra "Petra"), which is located in modern-day [Jordan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jordan "Jordan"), was the capital of the Nabataean kingdom and is renowned for its impressive rock-cut architecture.<sup id="cite_ref-469"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-469">[466]</a></sup>

Prior to the start of the [Arab conquests](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Early_Muslim_conquests "Early Muslim conquests"), Arab tribal client states, the [Lakhmids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lakhmid_kingdom "Lakhmid kingdom") and [Ghassanids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghassanids "Ghassanids"), were located on the borders of the Sassanid and Byzantine empires and were exposed to the cultural and architectural influences of both.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShahîd1995a401–403_470-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShah%C3%AEd1995a401%E2%80%93403-470">[467]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFloodNecipoğlu201758_471-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFloodNecipo%C4%9Flu201758-471">[468]</a></sup> They most likely played a significant role in transmitting and adapting the architectural traditions of these two empires to the later Arab Islamic dynasties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShahîd1995a391,_402_472-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShah%C3%AEd1995a391,_402-472">[469]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEttinghausenGrabarJenkins-Madina20014–5_473-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEttinghausenGrabarJenkins-Madina20014%E2%80%935-473">[470]</a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Jerusalem-2013-Temple_Mount-Dome_of_the_Rock_%26_Chain_02.jpg/198px-Jerusalem-2013-Temple_Mount-Dome_of_the_Rock_%26_Chain_02.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Jerusalem-2013-Temple_Mount-Dome_of_the_Rock_%26_Chain_02.jpg)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Alhambra_%2851949319806%29.jpg/186px-Alhambra_%2851949319806%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Alhambra_(51949319806).jpg)

The Dome of the Rock located in Jerusalem, Alhambra is a palace and fortress complex located in Granada, Andalusia, Spain.

The Arab empire expanded rapidly, and with it, came a diverse range of architectural influences. One of the most notable architectural achievements of the Arab Empire is the [Great Mosque of Damascus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Umayyad_Mosque "Umayyad Mosque") in Syria, which was built in the early 8th century, was constructed on the site of a Christian basilica and incorporated elements of Byzantine and Roman architecture, such as arches, columns, and intricate mosaics. Another important architectural is the [Al-Aqsa Mosque](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Aqsa_Mosque "Al-Aqsa Mosque") in [Jerusalem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jerusalem "Jerusalem"), which was built in the late 7th century. The mosque features an impressive dome and a large prayer hall, as well as intricate geometric patterns and calligraphy on the walls.<sup id="cite_ref-474"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-474">[471]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Krautheimer2_475-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Krautheimer2-475">[472]</a></sup>

### Music

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Umm_Kulthum4.jpg/170px-Umm_Kulthum4.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Umm_Kulthum4.jpg)

[Umm Kulthum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Umm_Kulthum "Umm Kulthum") was an Arab singer, [songwriter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Songwriter "Songwriter"), and film actress (1920s-1970s). She has been named among the “200 Greatest Singers of All Time”.<sup id="cite_ref-476"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-476">[473]</a></sup>

[Arabic music](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic_music "Arabic music"), while independent and flourishing in the 2010s, has a long history of interaction with many other regional musical styles and genres. It is an amalgam of the music of the Arab people in the Arabian Peninsula and the music of all the peoples that make up the Arab world today.<sup id="cite_ref-477"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-477">[474]</a></sup> Pre-Islamic Arab music was similar to that of Ancient Middle Eastern music. Most historians agree that there existed distinct forms of music in the [Arabian peninsula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabian_peninsula "Arabian peninsula") in the pre-Islamic period between the 5th and 7th century CE. [Arab poets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic_poetry "Arabic poetry") of that "Jahili poets", meaning "the poets of the period of ignorance"—used to recite poems with a high notes.<sup id="cite_ref-Jahili2_478-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Jahili2-478">[475]</a></sup> It was believed that [Jinns](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genie "Genie") revealed poems to poets and music to musicians.<sup id="cite_ref-Jahili2_478-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Jahili2-478">[475]</a></sup> By the 11th century, Islamic Iberia had become a center for the manufacture of instruments. These goods spread gradually throughout [France](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/France "France"), influencing French [troubadours](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Troubadour "Troubadour"), and eventually reaching the rest of [Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Europe "Europe"). The English words [lute](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lute "Lute"), [rebec](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rebec "Rebec"), and [naker](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naqareh "Naqareh") are derived from Arabic [oud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oud "Oud"), [rabab](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rebab "Rebab"), and [naqareh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naqareh "Naqareh").<sup id="cite_ref-479"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-479">[476]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-480"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-480">[477]</a></sup>

A number of [musical instruments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Musical_instrument "Musical instrument") used in [classical music](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_music "Classical music") are believed to have been derived from Arabic musical instruments: the [lute](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lute "Lute") was derived from the _[Oud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oud "Oud")_, the [rebec](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rebec "Rebec") (ancestor of [violin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Violin "Violin")) from the _[Maghreb rebab](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maghreb_rebab "Maghreb rebab")_, the [guitar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guitar "Guitar") from _qitara_, which in turn was derived from the Persian [Tar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tar_(lute) "Tar (lute)"), [naker](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naqareh "Naqareh") from _[naqareh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naqareh "Naqareh")_, [adufe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adufe "Adufe") from _[al-duff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Daf "Daf")_, [alboka](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alboka "Alboka") from _al-buq_, _anafil_ from _[al-nafir](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nafir_(trumpet) "Nafir (trumpet)")_, exabeba from _al-shabbaba_ ([flute](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flute "Flute")), atabal ([bass drum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bass_drum "Bass drum")) from _al-tabl_, atambal from _al-tinbal_,<sup id="cite_ref-481"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-481">[478]</a></sup> the [balaban](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Balaban_(instrument) "Balaban (instrument)"), the [castanet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Castanet "Castanet") from _kasatan_, [sonajas de azófar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tuna_(music) "Tuna (music)") from _sunuj al-sufr_, the [conical bore](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bore_(wind_instruments) "Bore (wind instruments)") [wind instruments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wind_instrument "Wind instrument"),<sup id="cite_ref-482"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-482">[479]</a></sup> the xelami from the _sulami_ or _[fistula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fistula "Fistula")_ (flute or [musical pipe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Organ_pipe "Organ pipe")),<sup id="cite_ref-483"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-483">[480]</a></sup> the [shawm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shawm "Shawm") and [dulzaina](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dulzaina "Dulzaina") from the [reed instruments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reed_(instrument) "Reed (instrument)") _zamr_ and _[al-zurna](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zurna "Zurna")_,<sup id="cite_ref-484"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-484">[481]</a></sup> the [gaita](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galician_gaita "Galician gaita") from the _[ghaita](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhaita "Rhaita")_, [rackett](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rackett "Rackett") from _iraqya_ or _iraqiyya_,<sup id="cite_ref-485"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-485">[482]</a></sup> [geige](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Violin "Violin") (violin) from _ghichak_,<sup id="cite_ref-486"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-486">[483]</a></sup> and the [theorbo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theorbo "Theorbo") from the _tarab_.<sup id="cite_ref-487"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-487">[484]</a></sup>

During the 1950s and the 1960s, Arabic music began to take on a more Western tone – artists [Umm Kulthum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Umm_Kulthum "Umm Kulthum"), [Abdel Halim Hafez](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abdel_Halim_Hafez "Abdel Halim Hafez"), and [Shadia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shadia "Shadia") along with composers [Mohamed Abd al-Wahab](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mohammed_Abdel_Wahab "Mohammed Abdel Wahab") and [Baligh Hamdi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baligh_Hamdi "Baligh Hamdi") pioneered the use of western instruments in Egyptian music. By the 1970s several other singers had followed suit and a strand of [Arabic pop](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic_pop "Arabic pop") was born. Arabic pop usually consists of Western styled songs with Arabic instruments and lyrics. Melodies are often a mix between Eastern and Western. Beginning in the mid-1980s, [Lydia Canaan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lydia_Canaan "Lydia Canaan"), musical [pioneer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Innovator "Innovator") widely regarded as the first rock star of the [Middle East](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_East "Middle East")<sup id="cite_ref-Daily_Star_Rock_Hall2_488-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Daily_Star_Rock_Hall2-488">[485]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-489"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-489">[486]</a></sup>

### Spirituality

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Nemesis_Allat_and_dedicater-MBA_Lyon-IMG_0557.JPG/220px-Nemesis_Allat_and_dedicater-MBA_Lyon-IMG_0557.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Nemesis_Allat_and_dedicater-MBA_Lyon-IMG_0557.JPG)

Bas-relief: [Nemesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nemesis "Nemesis"), [al-Lat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Lat "Al-Lat") and the dedicator. [Palmyrene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palmyra "Palmyra"), 2nd–3rd century CE.

[Arab polytheism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Religion_in_pre-Islamic_Arabia "Religion in pre-Islamic Arabia") was the dominant religion in [pre-Islamic Arabia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pre-Islamic_Arabia "Pre-Islamic Arabia"). [Gods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deity "Deity") and [goddesses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Goddess "Goddess"), including [Hubal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubal "Hubal") and the goddesses [al-Lāt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Lat "Al-Lat"), [Al-'Uzzá](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-%27Uzz%C3%A1 "Al-'Uzzá") and [Manāt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Man%C4%81t "Manāt"), were worshipped at local shrines, such as the [Kaaba](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kaaba "Kaaba") in [Mecca](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mecca "Mecca"), whilst Arabs in the south, in what is today's Yemen, worshipped various gods, some of which represented the Sun or Moon. Different theories [have been proposed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Religion_in_pre-Islamic_Arabia#Mecca "Religion in pre-Islamic Arabia") regarding the role of [Allah](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allah "Allah") in Meccan religion.<sup id="cite_ref-Robinson2_490-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Robinson2-490">[487]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Peters2_491-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Peters2-491">[488]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Peterson20072_492-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Peterson20072-492">[489]</a></sup> Many of the physical descriptions of the pre-Islamic gods are traced to [idols](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cult_image "Cult image"), especially near the Kaaba, which is said to have contained up to 360 of them.<sup id="cite_ref-armstrong2_493-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-armstrong2-493">[490]</a></sup> Until about the fourth century, almost all Arabs practised polytheistic religions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoyland2002139_494-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoyland2002139-494">[491]</a></sup> Although significant [Jewish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Judaism "Judaism") and Christian minorities developed, [polytheism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polytheism "Polytheism") remained the dominant belief system in pre-Islamic Arabia.<sup id="cite_ref-Nicolle2_495-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Nicolle2-495">[492]</a></sup>

The religious beliefs and practices of the nomadic [bedouin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bedouin "Bedouin") were distinct from those of the settled tribes of towns such as [Mecca](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mecca "Mecca").<sup id="cite_ref-Aslan62_496-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Aslan62-496">[493]</a></sup> Nomadic religious belief systems and practices are believed to have included [fetishism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fetishism "Fetishism"), [totemism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Totemism "Totemism") and [veneration of the dead](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Veneration_of_the_dead "Veneration of the dead") but were connected principally with immediate concerns and problems and did not consider larger [philosophical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophy "Philosophy") questions such as the afterlife.<sup id="cite_ref-Aslan62_496-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Aslan62-496">[493]</a></sup> Settled urban Arabs, on the other hand, are thought to have believed in a more complex [pantheon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pantheon_(religion) "Pantheon (religion)") of deities.<sup id="cite_ref-Aslan62_496-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Aslan62-496">[493]</a></sup> While the Meccans and the other settled inhabitants of the [Hejaz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hejaz "Hejaz") worshipped their gods at permanent shrines in towns and oases, the bedouin practised their religion on the move.<sup id="cite_ref-Peters1052_497-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs#cite_note-Peters1052-497">[494]</a></sup>

Most notable Arab gods and goddesses: ['Amm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%27Amm "'Amm"), [A'ra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/A%27ra "A'ra"), [Abgal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abgal_(god) "Abgal (god)"), [Allah](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allah#Pre-Islamic_Arabians "Allah"), [Al-Lat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Lat "Al-Lat"), [Al-Qaum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Qaum "Al-Qaum"),
